</div>
<p><b>Spiders</b> (<a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Araneae</b>) are air-breathing <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> that have eight legs and <a href="/wiki/Chelicera" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicera">chelicerae</a> with fangs that inject <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venom</a>. They are the largest order of <a href="/wiki/Arachnida" class="mw-redirect" title="Arachnida">arachnids</a> and rank seventh in total species diversity among all other <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">orders</a> of organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-sebastin_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sebastin-2">[2]</a></sup> Spiders are found worldwide on every continent except for <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, and have become established in nearly every <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> with the exceptions of air and sea colonization. As of November 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Spider&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, at least 45,700 spider <a href="/wiki/Species_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Species (biology)">species</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and 114 <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> have been recorded by <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSC_stats_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSC_stats-1">[1]</a></sup> However, there has been dissension within the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a> as to how all these families should be classified, as evidenced by the over 20 different classifications that have been proposed since 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-Bioofspiders_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bioofspiders-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anatomically, spiders differ from other arthropods in that the usual body <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segments</a> are fused into two <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(biology)" title="Tagma (biology)">tagmata</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>, and joined by a small, cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Pedicel_(spider)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pedicel (spider)">pedicel</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, spiders do not have <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>. In all except the most primitive group, the <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a>, spiders have the most centralized nervous systems of all arthropods, as all their <a href="/wiki/Ganglia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a> are fused into one mass in the cephalothorax. Unlike most arthropods, spiders have no <a href="/wiki/Extensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Extensor">extensor</a> muscles in their limbs and instead extend them by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> pressure.</p>
<p>Their abdomens bear appendages that have been modified into <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a> that extrude <a href="/wiki/Spider_silk" title="Spider silk">silk</a> from up to six types of glands. <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">Spider webs</a> vary widely in size, shape and the amount of sticky thread used. It now appears that the spiral orb web may be one of the earliest forms, and spiders that produce tangled cobwebs are more abundant and diverse than <a href="/wiki/Orb-web_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Orb-web spider">orb-web spiders</a>. Spider-like <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a> with silk-producing <a href="/wiki/Spigot" class="mw-redirect" title="Spigot">spigots</a> appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Period_(geology)" title="Period (geology)">period</a> about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=386">386</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, but these animals apparently lacked spinnerets. True spiders have been found in <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> rocks from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=318–299">318&#160;to&#160;299</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, and are very similar to the most primitive surviving <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">suborder</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a>. The main groups of modern spiders, <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Araneomorphae" title="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</a>, first appeared in the Triassic period, before <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=200">200</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Herbivory" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbivory">herbivorous</a> species, <i><a href="/wiki/Bagheera_kiplingi" title="Bagheera kiplingi">Bagheera kiplingi</a></i>, was described in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-CBio2009_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBio2009-5">[5]</a></sup> but all other known species are <a href="/wiki/Predator" class="mw-redirect" title="Predator">predators</a>, mostly preying on insects and on other spiders, although a few large species also take birds and lizards. Spiders use a wide range of strategies to capture prey: trapping it in sticky webs, <a href="/wiki/Lasso" title="Lasso">lassoing</a> it with sticky <a href="/wiki/Bolas" title="Bolas">bolas</a>, mimicking the prey to avoid detection, or running it down. Most detect prey mainly by sensing vibrations, but the active hunters have acute vision, and hunters of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Portia_(spider)" title="Portia (spider)">Portia</a></i> show signs of intelligence in their choice of tactics and ability to develop new ones. Spiders' guts are too narrow to take solids, and they liquefy their food by flooding it with digestive <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and grinding it with the bases of their <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a>, as they do not have true jaws.</p>
<p>Male spiders identify themselves by a variety of complex <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">courtship</a> rituals to avoid being eaten by the females. Males of most species survive a few matings, limited mainly by their short life spans. Females weave silk egg-cases, each of which may contain hundreds of eggs. Females of many species care for their young, for example by carrying them around or by sharing food with them. A minority of species are social, building communal webs that may house anywhere from a few to 50,000 individuals. Social behavior ranges from precarious toleration, as in the <a href="/wiki/Latrodectus" title="Latrodectus">widow spiders</a>, to co-operative hunting and food-sharing. Although most spiders live for at most two years, <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">mygalomorph</a> spiders can live up to 25&#160;years in captivity.</p>
<p>While the venom of a few species is dangerous to humans, scientists are now researching the use of spider venom in medicine and as non-polluting <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>. Spider silk provides a combination of lightness, strength and <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elasticity</a> that is superior to that of synthetic materials, and spider silk genes have been inserted into <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> to see if these can be used as silk factories. As a result of their wide range of behaviors, spiders have become common symbols in art and <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a> symbolizing various combinations of patience, cruelty and creative powers. An abnormal fear of spiders is called <a href="/wiki/Arachnophobia" title="Arachnophobia">arachnophobia</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2>Contents</h2>
</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_anatomy" title="Spider anatomy">Spider anatomy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Body_plan">Body plan</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:592px;max-width:592px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:280px;max-width:280px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:398px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg/278px-Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg" width="278" height="399" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg/417px-Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg/556px-Palystes_castaneus_female_dorsum_numbered.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1930" data-file-height="2767" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Palystes castaneus</i> female<br />
dorsal aspect<br />
<p>&#160;1: pedipalp<br />
&#160;2: trichobothria<br />
&#160;3: carapace of prosoma (cephalothorax)<br />
&#160;4: opisthosoma (abdomen)<br />
&#160;5: eyes – AL (anterior lateral)<br />
&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;AM (anterior median)<br />
&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;PL (posterior lateral)<br />
&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;PM (posterior median)<br />
Leg segments:<br />
&#160;6: costa<br />
&#160;7: trochanter<br />
&#160;8: patella<br />
&#160;9: tibia<br />
10: metatarsus<br />
11: tarsus<br />
13: claw<br />
14: chelicera<br /></p>
&#160;</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:308px;max-width:308px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:398px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG/306px-Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG" width="306" height="399" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG/459px-Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG/612px-Palystes_superciliosus_female_ventral_annotation_numbers.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3408" data-file-height="4440" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Nos 1 to 14 as for dorsal aspect<br />
<p>15: sternum of prosoma<br />
16: pedicel (also called pedicle)<br />
17: book lung sac<br />
18: book lung stigma<br />
19: epigastric fold<br />
20: epigyne<br />
21: anterior spinneret<br />
22: posterior spinneret<br /></p>
I, II, III, IV = Leg numbers from anterior to posterior</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiders are <a href="/wiki/Chelicerate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicerate">chelicerates</a> and therefore <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen-6">[6]</a></sup> As arthropods they have: segmented bodies with jointed limbs, all covered in a <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> and proteins; heads that are composed of several segments that fuse during the development of the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-7">[7]</a></sup> Being chelicerates, their bodies consist of two <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(biology)" title="Tagma (biology)">tagmata</a>, sets of segments that serve similar functions: the foremost one, called the <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> or <a href="/wiki/Prosoma" class="mw-redirect" title="Prosoma">prosoma</a>, is a complete fusion of the segments that in an <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a> would form two separate tagmata, the <a href="/wiki/Head_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Head (anatomy)">head</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a>; the rear tagma is called the <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Opisthosoma" title="Opisthosoma">opisthosoma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen-6">[6]</a></sup> In spiders, the cephalothorax and abdomen are connected by a small cylindrical section, the pedicel.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> The pattern of segment fusion that forms chelicerates' heads is unique among arthropods, and what would normally be the first head segment disappears at an early stage of development, so that chelicerates lack the <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> typical of most arthropods. In fact, chelicerates' only <a href="/wiki/Appendage" title="Appendage">appendages</a> ahead of the mouth are a pair of <a href="/wiki/Chelicera" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicera">chelicerae</a>, and they lack anything that would function directly as "jaws".<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P518-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup> The first appendages behind the mouth are called <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a>, and serve different functions within different groups of chelicerates.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ChelicerataGen-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiders and <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a> are members of one chelicerate group, the <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup> Scorpions' chelicerae have three sections and are used in feeding.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Scorpions_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Scorpions-10">[10]</a></sup> Spiders' chelicerae have two sections and terminate in <a href="/wiki/Fang" title="Fang">fangs</a> that are generally <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venomous</a>, and fold away behind the upper sections while not in use. The upper sections generally have thick "beards" that filter solid lumps out of their food, as spiders can take only liquid food.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Scorpions' pedipalps generally form large claws for capturing prey,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Scorpions_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Scorpions-10">[10]</a></sup> while those of spiders are fairly small appendages whose bases also act as an extension of the mouth; in addition, those of male spiders have enlarged last sections used for <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> transfer.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In spiders, the cephalothorax and abdomen are joined by a small, cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Pedicel_(spider)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pedicel (spider)">pedicel</a>, which enables the abdomen to move independently when producing <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>. The upper surface of the cephalothorax is covered by a single, <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/convex" class="extiw" title="wikt:convex">convex</a> <a href="/wiki/Carapace" title="Carapace">carapace</a>, while the underside is covered by two rather flat plates. The abdomen is soft and egg-shaped. It shows no sign of segmentation, except that the primitive <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a>, whose living members are the <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a>, have segmented plates on the upper surface.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation_and_respiration">Circulation and respiration</span></h3>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="Spider internal anatomy-en.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/700px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png" width="700" height="307" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/1050px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg/1400px-Spider_internal_anatomy-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1148" data-file-height="503" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Like other arthropods, spiders are <a href="/wiki/Coelomate" class="mw-redirect" title="Coelomate">coelomates</a> in which the coelom is reduced to small areas round the reproductive and excretory systems. Its place is largely taken by a <a href="/wiki/Hemocoel" class="mw-redirect" title="Hemocoel">hemocoel</a>, a cavity that runs most of the length of the body and through which <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> flows. The heart is a tube in the upper part of the body, with a few ostia that act as non-return valves allowing blood to enter the heart from the hemocoel but prevent it from leaving before it reaches the front end.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P527To528-11">[11]</a></sup> However, in spiders, it occupies only the upper part of the abdomen, and blood is discharged into the hemocoel by one artery that opens at the rear end of the abdomen and by branching <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a> that pass through the pedicle and open into several parts of the cephalothorax. Hence spiders have open <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> The blood of many spiders that have <a href="/wiki/Book_lung" title="Book lung">book lungs</a> contains the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_pigment" title="Respiratory pigment">respiratory pigment</a> <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> transport more efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiders have developed several different respiratory anatomies, based on <a href="/wiki/Book_lungs" class="mw-redirect" title="Book lungs">book lungs</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate_trachea" class="mw-redirect" title="Invertebrate trachea">tracheal</a> system, or both. <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Mygalomorph">Mygalomorph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a> spiders have two pairs of <a href="/wiki/Book_lungs" class="mw-redirect" title="Book lungs">book lungs</a> filled with haemolymph, where openings on the <a href="/wiki/Ventral" class="mw-redirect" title="Ventral">ventral</a> surface of the abdomen allow air to enter and diffuse <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. This is also the case for some basal <a href="/wiki/Araneomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Araneomorph">araneomorph</a> spiders, like the family <a href="/wiki/Hypochilidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypochilidae">Hypochilidae</a>, but the remaining members of this group have just the anterior pair of book lungs intact while the posterior pair of breathing organs are partly or fully modified into tracheae, through which oxygen is diffused into the haemolymph or directly to the tissue and organs.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> The trachea system has most likely evolved in small ancestors to help resist <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup> The trachea were originally connected to the surroundings through a pair of openings called <a href="/wiki/Spiracle" title="Spiracle">spiracles</a>, but in the majority of spiders this pair of spiracles has fused into a single one in the middle, and moved backwards close to the <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Spiders that have tracheae generally have higher <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolic rate">metabolic rates</a> and better water conservation.<sup id="cite_ref-CoddingtonLevi1991_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoddingtonLevi1991-12">[12]</a></sup> Spiders are <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectotherms</a>, so environmental temperatures affect their activity.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding.2C_digestion_and_excretion">Feeding, digestion and excretion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg/220px-Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg/330px-Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg/440px-Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="534" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheiracanthium_punctorium_frei_1_17_Forst_Jungfernhdeide_Jg_46_070920.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Cheiracanthium_punctorium" title="Cheiracanthium punctorium">Cheiracanthium punctorium</a></i>, displaying fangs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Uniquely among <a href="/wiki/Chelicerate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicerate">chelicerates</a>, the final sections of spiders' <a href="/wiki/Chelicerae" title="Chelicerae">chelicerae</a> are fangs, and the great majority of spiders can use them to inject <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venom</a> into prey from venom <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a> in the roots of the chelicerae.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> The family <a href="/wiki/Uloboridae" title="Uloboridae">Uloboridae</a> has lost its venom glands, and kills its prey with silk instead. Like most <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup> spiders have a narrow gut that can only cope with liquid food and spiders have two sets of filters to keep solids out.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> They use one of two different systems of external digestion. Some pump digestive <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> from the midgut into the prey and then suck the liquified tissues of the prey into the gut, eventually leaving behind the empty husk of the prey. Others grind the prey to pulp using the chelicerae and the bases of the <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a>, while flooding it with enzymes; in these species, the chelicerae and the bases of the pedipalps form a preoral cavity that holds the food they are processing.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The stomach in the cephalothorax acts as a pump that sends the food deeper into the digestive system. The mid gut bears many digestive <a href="/wiki/Cecum" title="Cecum">ceca</a>, compartments with no other exit, that extract nutrients from the food; most are in the abdomen, which is dominated by the digestive system, but a few are found in the cephalothorax.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most spiders convert <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogenous</a> waste products into <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>, which can be excreted as a dry material. <a href="/wiki/Malphigian_tubules" class="mw-redirect" title="Malphigian tubules">Malphigian tubules</a> ("little tubes") extract these wastes from the blood in the hemocoel and dump them into the <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloacal</a> chamber, from which they are expelled through the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Production of uric acid and its removal via Malphigian tubules are a water-conserving feature that has evolved independently in several <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropod</a> lineages that can live far away from water,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-14">[14]</a></sup> for example the tubules of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and arachnids develop from completely different parts of the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup> However, a few primitive spiders, the sub-<a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a> and infra-order <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</a>, retain the ancestral arthropod <a href="/wiki/Nephridia" class="mw-redirect" title="Nephridia">nephridia</a> ("little <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>"),<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> which use large amounts of water to excrete nitrogenous waste products as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P529To530-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_nervous_system">Central nervous system</span></h3>
<p>The basic arthropod <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> consists of a pair of <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">nerve cords</a> running below the gut, with paired <a href="/wiki/Ganglia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a> as local control centers in all segments; a brain formed by fusion of the ganglia for the head segments ahead of and behind the mouth, so that the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> is encircled by this conglomeration of ganglia.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532-15">[15]</a></sup> Except for the primitive <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a> are the sole surviving family, spiders have the much more centralized nervous system that is typical of arachnids: <i>all</i> the ganglia of all segments behind the esophagus are fused, so that the cephalothorax is largely filled with nervous tissue and there are no ganglia in the abdomen;<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004ArachnidaGen-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P531To532-15">[15]</a></sup> in the Mesothelae, the ganglia of the abdomen and the rear part of the cephalothorax remain unfused.<sup id="cite_ref-CoddingtonLevi1991_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoddingtonLevi1991-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the relatively small central nervous system, some spiders (like <i><a href="/wiki/Portia_(spider)" title="Portia (spider)">Portia</a></i>) exhibit complex behaviour, including the ability to use a trial-and-error approach.<sup id="cite_ref-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sense_organs">Sense organs</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eyes">Eyes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clynotis_severus,_AF_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg/240px-Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg" width="240" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg/360px-Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg/480px-Clynotis_severus%2C_AF_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1850" data-file-height="1255" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clynotis_severus,_AF_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spider</a>'s main <a href="/wiki/Ocelli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a> (center pair) are very acute. The outer pair are "secondary eyes" and there are other pairs of secondary eyes on the sides and top of its head.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-18">[18]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most spiders have four pairs of eyes on the top-front area of the cephalothorax, arranged in patterns that vary from one family to another.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> The pair at the front are of the type called pigment-cup <a href="/wiki/Ocelli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a> ("little eyes"), which in most <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are only capable of detecting the direction from which light is coming, using the shadow cast by the walls of the cup. However, the main eyes at the front of spiders' heads are pigment-cup ocelli that are capable of forming images.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The other eyes are thought to be derived from the <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" class="mw-redirect" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> of the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Chelicerate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicerate">chelicerates</a>, but no longer have the separate facets typical of compound eyes. Unlike the main eyes, in many spiders these secondary eyes detect light reflected from a reflective <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">wolf spiders</a> can be spotted by torch light reflected from the tapeta. On the other hand, jumping spiders' secondary eyes have no tapeta.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Some jumping spiders' <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">visual acuity</a> exceeds by a factor of ten that of <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>, which have by far the best vision among <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>; in fact the human eye is only about five times sharper than a jumping spider's. They achieve this by a <a href="/wiki/Telephoto" class="mw-redirect" title="Telephoto">telephoto</a>-like series of lenses, a four-layer retina and the ability to swivel their eyes and integrate images from different stages in the scan. The downside is that the scanning and integrating processes are relatively slow.<sup id="cite_ref-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are spiders with a reduced number of eyes, of these those with <a href="/wiki/List_of_six-eyed_spiders" title="List of six-eyed spiders">six-eyes</a> are the most numerous and are missing a pair of eyes on the <a href="/wiki/Anterior_median_line" title="Anterior median line">anterior median line</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> others species have four-eyes and some just two. <a href="/wiki/List_of_troglobites" title="List of troglobites">Cave dwelling</a> species have no eyes, or possess vestigial eyes incapable of sight.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_senses">Other senses</span></h4>
<p>As with other arthropods, spiders' <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticles</a> would block out information about the outside world, except that they are penetrated by many sensors or connections from sensors to the nervous system. In fact, spiders and other arthropods have modified their cuticles into elaborate arrays of sensors. Various touch sensors, mostly bristles called <a href="/wiki/Seta" title="Seta">setae</a>, respond to different levels of force, from strong contact to very weak air currents. Chemical sensors provide equivalents of <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a>, often by means of setae.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-18">[18]</a></sup> Pedipalps carry a large number of such setae sensitive to contact chemicals and air-borne smells, such as female pheromones.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Spiders also have in the joints of their limbs <a href="/wiki/Slit_sensilla" title="Slit sensilla">slit sensillae</a> that detect forces and vibrations. In web-building spiders, all these mechanical and chemical sensors are more important than the eyes, while the eyes are most important to spiders that hunt actively.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like most arthropods, spiders lack balance and <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> sensors and rely on their eyes to tell them which way is up. Arthropods' <a href="/wiki/Proprioceptor" class="mw-redirect" title="Proprioceptor">proprioceptors</a>, sensors that report the force exerted by muscles and the degree of bending in the body and joints, are well understood. On the other hand, little is known about what other internal sensors spiders or other arthropods may have.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P532To537-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aranna_pata.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Aranna_pata.png/220px-Aranna_pata.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Aranna_pata.png 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="225" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aranna_pata.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Image of a spider leg: 1–coxa; 2–trochanter; 3–femur; 4–patella; 5–tibia; 6–metatarsus; 7–tarsus; 8–claws</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Each of the eight legs of a spider consists of seven distinct parts. The part closest to and attaching the leg to the cephalothorax is the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Coxa" title="Arthropod leg">coxa</a>; the next segment is the short <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Trochanter" title="Arthropod leg">trochanter</a> that works as a hinge for the following long segment, the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Femur" title="Arthropod leg">femur</a>; next is the spider's knee, the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#patella" title="Arthropod leg">patella</a>, which acts as the hinge for the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#Tibia" title="Arthropod leg">tibia</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#metatarsus" title="Arthropod leg">metatarsus</a> is next, and it connects the tibia to the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg#tarsus" title="Arthropod leg">tarsus</a> (which may be thought of as a foot of sorts); the tarsus ends in a claw made up of either two or three points, depending on the family to which the spider belongs. Although all arthropods use muscles attached to the inside of the exoskeleton to flex their limbs, spiders and a few other groups still use <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> pressure to extend them, a system inherited from their pre-arthropod ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The only <a href="/wiki/Extensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Extensor">extensor</a> muscles in spider legs are located in the three hip joints (bordering the coxa and the trochanter).<sup id="cite_ref-Weihmann2012_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihmann2012-23">[23]</a></sup> As a result, a spider with a punctured <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> cannot extend its legs, and the legs of dead spiders curl up.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Spiders can generate pressures up to eight times their resting level to extend their legs,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and jumping spiders can jump up to 50 times their own length by suddenly increasing the blood pressure in the third or fourth pair of legs.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Although larger spiders use hydraulics to straighten their legs, unlike smaller jumping spiders they depend on their <a href="/wiki/Flexor" class="mw-redirect" title="Flexor">flexor</a> muscles to generate the propulsive force for their jumps.<sup id="cite_ref-Weihmann2012_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihmann2012-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most spiders that hunt actively, rather than relying on webs, have dense tufts of fine hairs between the paired claws at the tips of their legs. These tufts, known as <a href="/wiki/Scopulae" title="Scopulae">scopulae</a>, consist of bristles whose ends are split into as many as 1,000 branches, and enable spiders with scopulae to walk up vertical glass and upside down on ceilings. It appears that scopulae get their grip from contact with extremely thin layers of water on surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Spiders, like most other <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>, keep at least four legs on the surface while walking or running.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2001ChelicerataInvertebrateZoology_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2001ChelicerataInvertebrateZoology-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silk_production">Silk production</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_silk" title="Spider silk">Spider silk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg/220px-Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg/330px-Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg/440px-Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Australian_orb_weaver_spinning_web.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Orb_weaver" class="mw-redirect" title="Orb weaver">orb weaver</a> producing silk from its spinnerets</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The abdomen has no appendages except those that have been modified to form one to four (usually three) pairs of short, movable <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a>, which emit <a href="/wiki/Spider_silk" title="Spider silk">silk</a>. Each spinneret has many <a href="/wiki/Spigot" class="mw-redirect" title="Spigot">spigots</a>, each of which is connected to one silk <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">gland</a>. There are at least six types of silk gland, each producing a different type of silk.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Silk is mainly composed of a <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> very similar to that used in insect silk. It is initially a liquid, and hardens not by exposure to air but as a result of being drawn out, which changes the internal structure of the protein.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> It is similar in <a href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" class="mw-redirect" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a> and biological materials such as <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, but is much more <a href="/wiki/Deformation_(engineering)" title="Deformation (engineering)">elastic</a>. In other words, it can stretch much further before breaking or losing shape.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some spiders have a <a href="/wiki/Cribellum" title="Cribellum">cribellum</a>, a modified spinneret with up to 40,000 spigots, each of which produces a single very fine fiber. The fibers are pulled out by the <a href="/wiki/Calamistrum" title="Calamistrum">calamistrum</a>, a comb-like set of bristles on the jointed tip of the cribellum, and combined into a composite woolly thread that is very effective in snagging the bristles of insects. The earliest spiders had cribella, which produced the first silk capable of capturing insects, before spiders developed silk coated with sticky droplets. However, most modern groups of spiders have lost the cribellum.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tarantulas also have silk glands in their feet.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even species that do not build webs to catch prey use silk in several ways: as wrappers for <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> and for fertilized eggs; as a "<a href="/wiki/Safety_harness" title="Safety harness">safety rope</a>"; for nest-building; and as "<a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachutes</a>" by the young of some species.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_life_cycle"><span id="Reproduction"></span>Reproduction and life cycle</span></h3>

Mating behaviour of <i><a href="/wiki/Filmy_dome_spider" title="Filmy dome spider">Neriene radiata</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg/170px-DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg/255px-DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg/340px-DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="934" data-file-height="1358" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_Nephila_clavipes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tiny male of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_orb_weaver" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden orb weaver">Golden orb weaver</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Nephila_clavipes" title="Nephila clavipes">Nephila clavipes</a></i>) (near the top of the leaf) is protected from the female by his producing the right vibrations in the web, and may be too small to be worth eating.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiders reproduce <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sexually</a> and fertilization is <a href="/wiki/Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization">internal</a> but indirect, in other words the <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> is not inserted into the female's body by the male's genitals but by an intermediate stage. Unlike many land-living <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P537To539-28">[28]</a></sup> male spiders do not produce ready-made <a href="/wiki/Spermatophore" title="Spermatophore">spermatophores</a> (packages of sperm), but spin small sperm webs on to which they <a href="/wiki/Ejaculate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ejaculate">ejaculate</a> and then transfer the sperm to special <a href="/wiki/Syringe" title="Syringe">syringe</a>-like structures, <a href="/wiki/Palpal_bulb" title="Palpal bulb">palpal bulbs</a> or palpal organs, borne on the tips of the <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a> of mature males. When a male detects signs of a female nearby he checks whether she is of the same species and whether she is ready to mate; for example in species that produce webs or "safety ropes", the male can identify the species and sex of these objects by "smell".<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiders generally use elaborate <a href="/wiki/Courtship" title="Courtship">courtship</a> rituals to prevent the large females from eating the small males before fertilization, except where the male is so much smaller that he is not worth eating. In web-weaving species, precise patterns of vibrations in the web are a major part of the rituals, while patterns of touches on the female's body are important in many spiders that hunt actively, and may "hypnotize" the female. Gestures and dances by the male are important for <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spiders</a>, which have excellent eyesight. If courtship is successful, the male injects his sperm from the palpal bulbs into the female's genital opening, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Epigyne" title="Epigyne">epigyne</a></i>, on the underside of her abdomen. Female's reproductive tracts vary from simple tubes to systems that include seminal receptacles in which females store sperm and release it when they are ready.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Males of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tidarren" title="Tidarren">Tidarren</a></i> amputate one of their palps before maturation and enter adult life with one palp only. The palps are 20% of male's body mass in this species, and detaching one of the two improves mobility. In the <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemeni</a> species <i>Tidarren argo</i>, the remaining palp is then torn off by the female. The separated palp remains attached to the female's epigynum for about four hours and apparently continues to function independently. In the meantime, the female feeds on the palpless male.<sup id="cite_ref-JoZo254_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoZo254-29">[29]</a></sup> In over 60% of cases, the female of the Australian <a href="/wiki/Latrodectus_hasselti" class="mw-redirect" title="Latrodectus hasselti">redback spider</a> kills and eats the male after it inserts its second palp into the female's genital opening; in fact, the males co-operate by trying to impale themselves on the females' fangs. Observation shows that most male redbacks never get an opportunity to mate, and the "lucky" ones increase the likely number of offspring by ensuring that the females are well-fed.<sup id="cite_ref-Andrade2003_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrade2003-30">[30]</a></sup> However, males of most species survive a few matings, limited mainly by their short life spans. Some even live for a while in their mates' webs.<sup id="cite_ref-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="font-size:95%; line-height:120%">

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 234px">
<div style="width: 234px">
