</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">Wolf spider</a> carrying its young on its abdomen</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Females lay up to 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a> in one or more silk egg sacs,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> which maintain a fairly constant <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> level.<sup id="cite_ref-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction-31">[31]</a></sup> In some species, the females die afterwards, but females of other species protect the sacs by attaching them to their webs, hiding them in nests, carrying them in the <a href="/wiki/Chelicerae" title="Chelicerae">chelicerae</a> or attaching them to the <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a> and dragging them along.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baby spiders pass all their <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larval</a> stages inside the egg and hatch as spiderlings, very small and sexually immature but similar in shape to adults. Some spiders care for their young, for example a <a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">wolf spider</a>'s brood cling to rough bristles on the mother's back,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> and females of some species respond to the "begging" behaviour of their young by giving them their prey, provided it is no longer struggling, or even <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitate</a> food.<sup id="cite_ref-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like other <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, spiders have to <a href="/wiki/Molt" class="mw-redirect" title="Molt">molt</a> to grow as their <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a> ("skin") cannot stretch.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524-32">[32]</a></sup> In some species males mate with newly molted females, which are too weak to be dangerous to the males.<sup id="cite_ref-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foelix1996SpidersReproduction-31">[31]</a></sup> Most spiders live for only one to two years, although some <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a> can live in captivity for over 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Foelix_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foelix-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg/220px-Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg/330px-Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg/440px-Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5041" data-file-height="7360" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Theraphosa_blondi_MHNT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Goliath_birdeater" title="Goliath birdeater">Goliath birdeater</a> (<i>Theraphosa blondi</i>), the largest spider</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiders occur in a large range of sizes. The smallest, <i><a href="/wiki/Patu_digua" title="Patu digua">Patu digua</a></i> from Colombia, are less than 0.37&#160;mm (0.015&#160;in) in body length. The largest and heaviest spiders occur among <a href="/wiki/Tarantulas" class="mw-redirect" title="Tarantulas">tarantulas</a>, which can have body lengths up to 90&#160;mm (3.5&#160;in) and leg spans up to 250&#160;mm (9.8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coloration">Coloration</span></h3>
<p>Only three classes of <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ommochrome" title="Ommochrome">ommochromes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bilin_(biochemistry)" title="Bilin (biochemistry)">bilins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>) have been identified in spiders, although other pigments have been detected but not yet characterized. <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">Melanins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pterin" title="Pterin">pterins</a>, very common in other animals, are apparently absent. In some species, the <a href="/wiki/Exocuticle" class="mw-redirect" title="Exocuticle">exocuticle</a> of the legs and prosoma is modified by a <a href="/wiki/Tanning" title="Tanning">tanning</a> process, resulting in brown coloration.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordGillespie1998_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordGillespie1998-35">[35]</a></sup> Bilins are found, for example, in <i><a href="/wiki/Micrommata_virescens" title="Micrommata virescens">Micrommata virescens</a></i>, resulting in its green color. Guanine is responsible for the white markings of the <a href="/wiki/European_garden_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="European garden spider">European garden spider</a> <i>Araneus diadematus</i>. It is in many species accumulated in specialized cells called <a href="/w/index.php?title=Guanocyte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Guanocyte (page does not exist)">guanocytes</a>. In genera such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tetragnatha" title="Tetragnatha">Tetragnatha</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Leucauge" title="Leucauge">Leucauge</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Argyrodes" title="Argyrodes">Argyrodes</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Theridiosoma" title="Theridiosoma">Theridiosoma</a></i>, guanine creates their silvery appearance. While guanine is originally an end-product of protein metabolism, its excretion can be blocked in spiders, leading to an increase in its storage.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordGillespie1998_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordGillespie1998-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structural_coloration" title="Structural coloration">Structural colors</a> occur in some species, which are the result of the diffraction, scattering or interference of light, for example by modified <a href="/wiki/Setae" class="mw-redirect" title="Setae">setae</a> or scales. The white prosoma of <i><a href="/wiki/Argiope_(spider)" title="Argiope (spider)">Argiope</a></i> results from hairs reflecting the light, <i><a href="/wiki/Lycosa" title="Lycosa">Lycosa</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Josa" title="Josa">Josa</a></i> both have areas of modified cuticle that act as light reflectors.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordGillespie1998_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordGillespie1998-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_behavior">Ecology and behavior</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-predatory_feeding">Non-predatory feeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG/220px-A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG/330px-A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG/440px-A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1530" data-file-height="1026" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_garden_spider_in_Chennai.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spider</a> seen in Chennai.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although spiders are generally regarded as predatory, the <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spider</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Bagheera_kiplingi" title="Bagheera kiplingi">Bagheera kiplingi</a></i> gets over 90% of its food from fairly solid plant material produced by <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">acacias</a> as part of a <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutually beneficial relationship</a> with a species of <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Juveniles of some spiders in the families <a href="/wiki/Anyphaenidae" title="Anyphaenidae">Anyphaenidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corinnidae" title="Corinnidae">Corinnidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clubionidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Clubionidae">Clubionidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomisidae" title="Thomisidae">Thomisidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salticidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Salticidae">Salticidae</a> feed on plant <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>. Laboratory studies show that they do so deliberately and over extended periods, and periodically clean themselves while feeding. These spiders also prefer sugar solutions to plain water, which indicates that they are seeking nutrients. Since many spiders are nocturnal, the extent of nectar consumption by spiders may have been underestimated. Nectar contains <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a> and minerals in addition to sugars, and studies have shown that other spider species live longer when nectar is available. Feeding on nectar avoids the risks of struggles with prey, and the costs of producing venom and digestive enzymes.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson2001_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson2001-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various species are known to feed on dead arthropods (scavenging), web silk, and their own shed exoskeletons. <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">Pollen</a> caught in webs may also be eaten, and studies have shown that young spiders have a better chance of survival if they have the opportunity to eat pollen. In captivity, several spider species are also known to feed on <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">marmalade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg_yolk" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg yolk">egg yolk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sausages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sausages">sausages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson2001_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson2001-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_capturing_prey">Methods of capturing prey</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">Spider web</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spider_house_leaf.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Spider_house_leaf.jpg/220px-Spider_house_leaf.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Spider_house_leaf.jpg/330px-Spider_house_leaf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Spider_house_leaf.jpg/440px-Spider_house_leaf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spider_house_leaf.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Phonognatha_graeffei" title="Phonognatha graeffei">Phonognatha graeffei</a></i> or leaf-curling spider's <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">web</a> serves both as a trap and as a way of making its home in a leaf.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The best-known method of prey capture is by means of sticky webs. Varying placement of webs allows different species of spider to trap different insects in the same area, for example flat horizontal webs trap insects that fly up from vegetation underneath while flat vertical webs trap insects in horizontal flight. Web-building spiders have poor vision, but are extremely sensitive to vibrations.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Females of the water spider <i><a href="/wiki/Argyroneta" class="mw-redirect" title="Argyroneta">Argyroneta</a> aquatica</i> build underwater "diving bell" webs that they fill with air and use for digesting prey, molting, mating and raising offspring. They live almost entirely within the bells, darting out to catch prey animals that touch the bell or the threads that anchor it.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> A few spiders use the surfaces of lakes and ponds as "webs", detecting trapped insects by the vibrations that these cause while struggling.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Net-casting spiders weave only small webs, but then manipulate them to trap prey. Those of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hyptiotes" title="Hyptiotes">Hyptiotes</a></i> and the family <a href="/wiki/Theridiosomatidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Theridiosomatidae">Theridiosomatidae</a> stretch their webs and then release them when prey strike them, but do not actively move their webs. Those of the family <a href="/wiki/Deinopidae" title="Deinopidae">Deinopidae</a> weave even smaller webs, hold them outstretched between their first two pairs of legs, and lunge and push the webs as much as twice their own body length to trap prey, and this move may increase the webs' area by a factor of up to ten. Experiments have shown that <i><a href="/wiki/Deinopis" title="Deinopis">Deinopis</a> spinosus</i> has two different techniques for trapping prey: backwards strikes to catch flying insects, whose vibrations it detects; and forward strikes to catch ground-walking prey that it sees. These two techniques have also been observed in other deinopids. Walking insects form most of the prey of most deinopids, but one population of <i><a href="/wiki/Deinopis_subrufa" title="Deinopis subrufa">Deinopis subrufa</a></i> appears to live mainly on <a href="/wiki/Tipulidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Tipulidae">tipulid</a> flies that they catch with the backwards strike.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mature female <a href="/wiki/Bolas_spider" title="Bolas spider">bolas spiders</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Mastophora_(spider)" title="Mastophora (spider)">Mastophora</a></i> build "webs" that consist of only a single "trapeze line", which they patrol. They also construct a <a href="/wiki/Bolas" title="Bolas">bolas</a> made of a single thread, tipped with a large ball of very wet sticky silk. They emit chemicals that resemble the <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a>, and then swing the bolas at the moths. Although they miss on about 50% of strikes, they catch about the same weight of insects per night as web-weaving spiders of similar size. The spiders eat the bolas if they have not made a kill in about 30 minutes, rest for a while, and then make new bolas.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Juveniles and adult males are much smaller and do not make bolas. Instead they release different pheromones that attract <a href="/wiki/Moth_fly" class="mw-redirect" title="Moth fly">moth flies</a>, and catch them with their front pairs of legs.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_(Marshal_Hedin).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg/220px-Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg/330px-Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg/440px-Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_%28Marshal_Hedin%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyclocosmia_sp._in_burrow_(Marshal_Hedin).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A trapdoor spider in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Cyclocosmia" title="Cyclocosmia">Cyclocosmia</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">ambush predator</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The primitive <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a>, the "trapdoor spiders" of the family <a href="/wiki/Ctenizidae" title="Ctenizidae">Ctenizidae</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a> are <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">ambush predators</a> that lurk in burrows, often closed by trapdoors and often surrounded by networks of silk threads that alert these spiders to the presence of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-CoddingtonLevi1991_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoddingtonLevi1991-12">[12]</a></sup> Other ambush predators do without such aids, including many crab spiders,<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> and a few species that prey on <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, which see <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, can adjust their ultraviolet reflectance to match the flowers in which they are lurking.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordGillespie1998_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordGillespie1998-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">Wolf spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishing_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Fishing spider">fishing spiders</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Crab_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Crab spider">crab spiders</a> capture prey by chasing it, and rely mainly on vision to locate prey.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg/200px-Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg" width="200" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg/300px-Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portia.fimbriata.female.in.its.web.-.tanikawa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Portia_(spider)" title="Portia (spider)">Portia</a></i> uses both webs and cunning, versatile tactics to overcome prey.<sup id="cite_ref-WilcoxJackson2004JumpingSpiderTricksters_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WilcoxJackson2004JumpingSpiderTricksters-43">[43]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some jumping spiders of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Portia_(spider)" title="Portia (spider)">Portia</a></i> hunt other spiders in ways that seem intelligent,<sup id="cite_ref-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats-16">[16]</a></sup> outflanking their victims or luring them from their webs. Laboratory studies show that <i>Portia'</i>s instinctive tactics are only starting points for a <a href="/wiki/Trial-and-error" class="mw-redirect" title="Trial-and-error">trial-and-error</a> approach from which these spiders learn very quickly how to overcome new prey species.<sup id="cite_ref-WilcoxJackson2004JumpingSpiderTricksters_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WilcoxJackson2004JumpingSpiderTricksters-43">[43]</a></sup> However, they seem to be relatively slow "thinkers", which is not surprising, as their brains are vastly smaller than those of mammalian predators.<sup id="cite_ref-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HarlandJackson2000EightLeggedCats-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg/220px-Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg/330px-Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg/440px-Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg 2x" data-file-width="921" data-file-height="574" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ant_Mimic_Spider.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Myrmarachne" title="Myrmarachne">ant-mimicking jumping spider</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ant_mimicry" title="Ant mimicry">Ant-mimicking</a> spiders face several challenges: they generally develop slimmer abdomens and false "waists" in the <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> to mimic the three distinct regions (tagmata) of an ant's body; they wave the first pair of legs in front of their heads to mimic <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>, which spiders lack, and to conceal the fact that they have eight legs rather than six; they develop large color patches round one pair of eyes to disguise the fact that they generally have eight simple eyes, while ants have two compound eyes; they cover their bodies with reflective hairs to resemble the shiny bodies of ants. In some spider species, males and females mimic different ant species, as female spiders are usually much larger than males. Ant-mimicking spiders also modify their behavior to resemble that of the target species of ant; for example, many adopt a zig-zag pattern of movement, ant-mimicking jumping spiders avoid jumping, and spiders of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Synemosyna" title="Synemosyna">Synemosyna</a></i> walk on the outer edges of leaves in the same way as <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomyrmex" title="Pseudomyrmex">Pseudomyrmex</a></i>. Ant-mimicry in many spiders and other arthropods may be for protection from predators that hunt by sight, including birds, lizards and spiders. However, several ant-mimicking spiders prey either on ants or on the ants' "<a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>", such as <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>. When at rest, the ant-mimicking crab spider <i><a href="/wiki/Amyciaea" title="Amyciaea">Amyciaea</a></i> does not closely resemble <i><a href="/wiki/Oecophylla" class="mw-redirect" title="Oecophylla">Oecophylla</a></i>, but while hunting it imitates the behavior of a dying ant to attract worker ants. After a kill, some ant-mimicking spiders hold their victims between themselves and large groups of ants to avoid being attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense">Defense</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atrax_robustus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Atrax_robustus.jpg/220px-Atrax_robustus.jpg" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Atrax_robustus.jpg/330px-Atrax_robustus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Atrax_robustus.jpg/440px-Atrax_robustus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="434" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atrax_robustus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Threat display by a <a href="/wiki/Sydney_funnel-web_spider" title="Sydney funnel-web spider">Sydney funnel-web spider</a> (<i>Atrax robustus</i>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is strong evidence that spiders' coloration is <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a> that helps them to evade their major predators, <a href="/wiki/Birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_wasp" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasitic wasp">parasitic wasps</a>, both of which have good color vision. Many spider species are colored so as to merge with their most common backgrounds, and some have <a href="/wiki/Disruptive_coloration" title="Disruptive coloration">disruptive coloration</a>, stripes and blotches that break up their outlines. In a few species, such as the Hawaiian happy-face spider, <i><a href="/wiki/Theridion_grallator" title="Theridion grallator">Theridion grallator</a></i>, several coloration schemes are present in a ratio that appears to remain constant, and this may make it more difficult for predators to recognize the species. Most spiders are insufficiently dangerous or unpleasant-tasting for <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">warning coloration</a> to offer much benefit. However, a few species with powerful venoms, large jaws or irritant hairs have patches of warning colors, and some actively display these colors when threatened.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordGillespie1998_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordGillespie1998-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of the family <a href="/wiki/Theraphosidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Theraphosidae">Theraphosidae</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baboon_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Baboon spider">baboon spiders</a>, have <a href="/wiki/Urticating_hair" title="Urticating hair">urticating hairs</a> on their abdomens and use their legs to flick them at attackers. These hairs are fine <a href="/wiki/Seta" title="Seta">setae</a> (bristles) with fragile bases and a row of barbs on the tip. The barbs cause intense irritation but there is no evidence that they carry any kind of venom.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> A few defend themselves against wasps by including networks of very robust threads in their webs, giving the spider time to flee while the wasps are struggling with the obstacles.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The golden wheeling spider, <i><a href="/wiki/Wheel_spider" title="Wheel spider">Carparachne aureoflava</a></i>, of the Namibian desert escapes parasitic wasps by flipping onto its side and <a href="/wiki/Cartwheel_(gymnastics)" title="Cartwheel (gymnastics)">cartwheeling</a> down sand <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_spiders">Social spiders</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_spider" title="Social spider">Social spider</a></div>
<p>A few spider species that build webs live together in large colonies and show social behavior, although not as complex as in <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">social insects</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Anelosimus" title="Anelosimus">Anelosimus</a> <a href="/wiki/Anelosimus_eximius" title="Anelosimus eximius">eximius</a></i> (in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Theridiidae" title="Theridiidae">Theridiidae</a>) can form colonies of up to 50,000 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The genus <i>Anelosimus</i> has a strong tendency towards sociality: all known American species are social, and species in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> are at least somewhat social.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Members of other species in the same family but several different genera have <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">independently</a> developed social behavior. For example, although <i><a href="/wiki/Theridion" title="Theridion">Theridion</a> nigroannulatum</i> belongs to a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> with no other social species, <i>T. nigroannulatum</i> build colonies that may contain several thousand individuals that co-operate in prey capture and share food.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Other communal spiders include several <i><a href="/wiki/Philoponella" title="Philoponella">Philoponella</a></i> species (family <a href="/wiki/Uloboridae" title="Uloboridae">Uloboridae</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Agelena_consociata" title="Agelena consociata">Agelena consociata</a></i> (family <a href="/wiki/Agelenidae" title="Agelenidae">Agelenidae</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Mallos_(spider)" title="Mallos (spider)">Mallos</a> <a href="/wiki/Mallos_gregalis" title="Mallos gregalis">gregalis</a></i> (family <a href="/wiki/Dictynidae" title="Dictynidae">Dictynidae</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Social predatory spiders need to defend their prey against <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kleptoparasite">kleptoparasites</a> ("thieves"), and larger colonies are more successful in this.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The herbivorous spider <i><a href="/wiki/Bagheera_kiplingi" title="Bagheera kiplingi">Bagheera kiplingi</a></i> lives in small colonies which help to protect eggs and spiderlings.<sup id="cite_ref-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider-36">[36]</a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/Widow_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Widow spider">widow spiders</a> (genus <i>Latrodectus</i>), which are notoriously cannibalistic, have formed small colonies in captivity, sharing webs and feeding together.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Web_types">Web types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg/220px-Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg/330px-Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg/440px-Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Araneus_diadematus_web_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The large orb web of <i><a href="/wiki/Araneus_diadematus" title="Araneus diadematus">Araneus diadematus</a></i> (European garden spider).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">Spider web</a></div>
<p>There is no consistent relationship between the classification of spiders and the types of web they build: species in the same <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> may build very similar or significantly different webs. Nor is there much correspondence between spiders' classification and the chemical composition of their silks. <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">Convergent evolution</a> in web construction, in other words use of similar techniques by remotely related species, is rampant. Orb web designs and the spinning behaviors that produce them are the best understood. The basic radial-then-spiral sequence visible in orb webs and the sense of direction required to build them may have been inherited from the common ancestors of most spider groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs-55">[55]</a></sup> However, the majority of spiders build non-orb webs. It used to be thought that the sticky orb web was an evolutionary innovation resulting in the diversification of the <a href="/wiki/Orbiculariae" title="Orbiculariae">Orbiculariae</a>. Now, however, it appears that non-orb spiders are a sub-group that evolved from orb-web spiders, and non-orb spiders have over 40% more species and are four times as abundant as orb-web spiders. Their greater success may be because <a href="/wiki/Sphecid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sphecid">sphecid</a> <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, which are often the dominant predators of spiders, much prefer to attack spiders that have flat webs.<sup id="cite_ref-Agnarsson2004MorphologicalPhylogenyOfCobwebSpiders_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agnarsson2004MorphologicalPhylogenyOfCobwebSpiders-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orb_webs">Orb webs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg/220px-NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg/330px-NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg/440px-NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg 2x" data-file-width="660" data-file-height="820" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NephilaClavata-2005Kurihara.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Nephila_clavata" title="Nephila clavata">Nephila clavata</a></i>, a golden orb weaver</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>About half the potential prey that hit orb webs escape. A web has to perform three functions: intercepting the prey (intersection), absorbing its <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> without breaking (stopping), and trapping the prey by entangling it or sticking to it (retention). No single design is best for all prey. For example: wider spacing of lines will increase the web's area and hence its ability to intercept prey, but reduce its stopping power and retention; closer spacing, larger sticky droplets and thicker lines would improve retention, but would make it easier for potential prey to see and avoid the web, at least during the day. However, there are no consistent differences between orb webs built for use during the day and those built for use at night. In fact, there is no simple relationship between orb web design features and the prey they capture, as each orb-weaving species takes a wide range of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hubs of orb webs, where the spiders lurk, are usually above the center, as the spiders can move downwards faster than upwards. If there is an obvious direction in which the spider can retreat to avoid its own predators, the hub is usually offset towards that direction.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Horizontal orb webs are fairly common, despite being less effective at intercepting and retaining prey and more vulnerable to damage by rain and falling debris. Various researchers have suggested that horizontal webs offer compensating advantages, such as reduced vulnerability to wind damage; reduced visibility to prey flying upwards, because of the back-lighting from the sky; enabling <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillations</a> to catch insects in slow horizontal flight. However, there is no single explanation for the common use of horizontal orb webs.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiders often attach highly visible silk bands, called decorations or stabilimenta, to their webs. Field research suggests that webs with more decorative bands captured more prey per hour.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> However, a laboratory study showed that spiders reduce the building of these decorations if they sense the presence of predators.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are several unusual variants of orb web, many of them convergently evolved, including: attachment of lines to the surface of water, possibly to trap insects in or on the surface; webs with twigs through their centers, possibly to hide the spiders from predators; "ladder-like" webs that appear most effective in catching <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a>. However, the significance of many variations is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberhard1990FunctionAndPhylogenyOfSpiderWebs-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Skylab_3" title="Skylab 3">Skylab 3</a> took two orb-web spiders into <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> to test their web-spinning capabilities in zero gravity. At first, both produced rather sloppy webs, but they adapted quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tangleweb_spiders_.28cobweb_spiders.29">Tangleweb spiders (cobweb spiders)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG/150px-Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG" width="150" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG/225px-Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG/300px-Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1712" data-file-height="1368" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Agelenidae_labyrinthica.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A funnel web.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Members of the family <a href="/wiki/Theridiidae" title="Theridiidae">Theridiidae</a> weave irregular, tangled, three-dimensional webs, popularly known as cobwebs. There seems to be an evolutionary trend towards a reduction in the amount of sticky silk used, leading to its total absence in some species. The construction of cobwebs is less stereotyped than that of orb-webs, and may take several days.<sup id="cite_ref-Agnarsson2004MorphologicalPhylogenyOfCobwebSpiders_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agnarsson2004MorphologicalPhylogenyOfCobwebSpiders-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_types_of_webs">Other types of webs</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Linyphiidae" title="Linyphiidae">Linyphiidae</a> generally make horizontal but uneven sheets, with tangles of stopping threads above. Insects that hit the stopping threads fall onto the sheet or are shaken onto it by the spider, and are held by sticky threads on the sheet until the spider can attack from below.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Spider evolution">Spider evolution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spider_in_amber_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg/150px-Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg" width="150" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg/225px-Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg/300px-Spider_in_amber_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2317" data-file-height="1878" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spider_in_amber_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spider preserved in <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the fossil record of spiders is considered poor,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> almost 1000 species have been described from fossils.<sup id="cite_ref-dunlop_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunlop-62">[62]</a></sup> Because spiders' bodies are quite soft, the vast majority of fossil spiders have been found preserved in <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dunlop_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunlop-62">[62]</a></sup> The oldest known amber that contains fossil arthropods dates from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=130">130</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> in the Early <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period. In addition to preserving spiders' anatomy in very fine detail, pieces of amber show spiders mating, killing prey, producing silk and possibly caring for their young. In a few cases, amber has preserved spiders' egg sacs and webs, occasionally with prey attached;<sup id="cite_ref-PenneySelden2007SpinningWithDinosaurs_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PenneySelden2007SpinningWithDinosaurs-63">[63]</a></sup> the oldest fossil web found so far is 100&#160;million years old.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Earlier spider fossils come from a few <a href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tte" title="Lagerstätte">lagerstätten</a>, places where conditions were exceptionally suited to preserving fairly soft tissues.<sup id="cite_ref-PenneySelden2007SpinningWithDinosaurs_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PenneySelden2007SpinningWithDinosaurs-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palaeotarbus_jerami.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Palaeotarbus_jerami.png/150px-Palaeotarbus_jerami.png" width="150" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9e/Palaeotarbus_jerami.png 1.5x" data-file-width="188" data-file-height="174" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palaeotarbus_jerami.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Palaeotarbus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Palaeotarbus (page does not exist)">Palaeotarbus</a> jerami</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Trigonotarbid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonotarbid">trigonotarbid</a> and the oldest known exclusively terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnid</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest known exclusively terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnid</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Trigonotarbid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonotarbid">trigonotarbid</a> <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Palaeotarbus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Palaeotarbus (page does not exist)">Palaeotarbus</a> jerami</i>, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=420">420</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> in the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period, and had a triangular <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> and segmented abdomen, as well as eight legs and a pair of <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Attercopus" title="Attercopus">Attercopus</a> fimbriunguis</i>, from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=386">386</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> in the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period, bears the earliest known silk-producing spigots, and was therefore hailed as a spider at the time of its discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-66">[66]</a></sup> However, these spigots may have been mounted on the underside of the abdomen rather than on <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a>, which are modified <a href="/wiki/Appendage" title="Appendage">appendages</a> and whose mobility is important in the building of webs. Hence <i>Attercopus</i> and the similar <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> arachnid <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Permarachne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Permarachne (page does not exist)">Permarachne</a></i> may not have been true spiders, and probably used silk for lining nests or producing egg-cases rather than for building webs.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The largest known fossil spider as of 2011 is the <a href="/wiki/Araneid" class="mw-redirect" title="Araneid">araneid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Nephila_jurassica" class="mw-redirect" title="Nephila jurassica">Nephila jurassica</a></i>, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=165">165</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, recorded from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Daohuogo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Daohuogo (page does not exist)">Daohuogo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> in China.<sup id="cite_ref-Nephila_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nephila-68">[68]</a></sup> Its body length is almost 25&#160;mm, (i.e., almost one inch).</p>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> spiders were members of the <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a>, a primitive group now represented only by the <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-66">[66]</a></sup> The mesothelid <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleothele&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Paleothele (page does not exist)">Paleothele</a> montceauensis</i>, from the Late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> over <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=299">299</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, had five spinnerets.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> period <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=299–251">299&#160;to&#160;251</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span> saw rapid <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">diversification</a> of flying <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, there are very few fossil spiders from this period.<sup id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main groups of modern spiders, <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Araneomorphae" title="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</a>, first appear in the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> well before <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=200">200</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>. Some Triassic mygalomorphs appear to be members of the family <a href="/wiki/Hexathelidae" title="Hexathelidae">Hexathelidae</a>, whose modern members include the notorious <a href="/wiki/Sydney_funnel-web_spider" title="Sydney funnel-web spider">Sydney funnel-web spider</a>, and their spinnerets appear adapted for building funnel-shaped webs to catch jumping insects. <a href="/wiki/Araneomorphae" title="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</a> account for the great majority of modern spiders, including those that weave the familiar orb-shaped webs. The <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods provide a large number of fossil spiders, including representatives of many modern families.<sup id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-66">[66]</a></sup></p>

Shultz (2007)'s evolutionary family tree of <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a><sup id="cite_ref-Shultz2007_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz2007-70">[70]</a></sup> – <b>†</b> marks extinct groups.</div>
<p>It is now agreed that spiders (Araneae) are <a href="/wiki/Monophyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> (i.e., members of a group of organisms that form a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, consisting of a last common ancestor and all of its descendants).<sup id="cite_ref-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny-71">[71]</a></sup> There has been debate about what their closest evolutionary relatives are, and how all of these evolved from the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Chelicerate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicerate">chelicerates</a>, which were marine animals. The <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> on the right is based on J. W. Shultz' analysis (2007). Other views include proposals that: <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a> are more closely related to the extinct marine scorpion-like <a href="/wiki/Eurypterid" title="Eurypterid">eurypterids</a> than to spiders; spiders and <a href="/wiki/Amblypygi" title="Amblypygi">Amblypygi</a> are a monophyletic group. The appearance of several multi-way branchings in the tree on the right shows that there are still uncertainties about relationships between the groups involved.<sup id="cite_ref-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arachnids lack some features of other chelicerates, including backward-pointing mouths and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gnathobase&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gnathobase (page does not exist)">gnathobases</a> ("jaw bases") at the bases of their legs;<sup id="cite_ref-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny-71">[71]</a></sup> both of these features are part of the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropod</a> feeding system.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Instead, they have mouths that point forwards and downwards, and all have some means of breathing air.<sup id="cite_ref-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shultz2007ArachnidPhylogeny-71">[71]</a></sup> Spiders (Araneae) are distinguished from other arachnid groups by several characteristics, including <a href="/wiki/Spinneret_(spider)" title="Spinneret (spider)">spinnerets</a> and, in males, <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a> that are specially adapted for sperm transfer.<sup id="cite_ref-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_taxonomy" title="Spider taxonomy">Spider taxonomy</a></div>
<p>Spiders are divided into two suborders, <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opisthothelae" title="Opisthothelae">Opisthothelae</a>, of which the latter contains two infraorders, <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Araneomorphae" title="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</a>. Nearly 46,000 living <a href="/wiki/Species_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Species (biology)">species</a> of spiders (order Araneae) have been identified and are currently grouped into about 114 <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> and about 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> by <a href="/wiki/Arachnology" title="Arachnology">arachnologists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSC_stats_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSC_stats-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
<tr>
<td>&#160;</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg/150px-Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg" width="150" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg/225px-Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg/300px-Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="385" data-file-height="262" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryuthela.secundaria.female.-.takinawa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Ryuthela_secundaria" title="Ryuthela secundaria">Ryuthela secundaria</a></i>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesothelae" title="Mesothelae">Mesothelae</a></div>
<p>The only living members of the primitive Mesothelae are the family <a href="/wiki/Liphistiidae" title="Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a>, found only in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders-73">[73]</a></sup> Most of the Liphistiidae construct silk-lined burrows with thin <a href="/wiki/Trapdoor" title="Trapdoor">trapdoors</a>, although some species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Liphistius" title="Liphistius">Liphistius</a></i> build camouflaged silk tubes with a second trapdoor as an emergency exit. Members of the genus <i>Liphistius</i> run silk "<a href="/wiki/Tripwire" title="Tripwire">tripwires</a>" outwards from their tunnels to help them detect approaching prey, while those of genus <i><a href="/wiki/Heptathela" title="Heptathela">Heptathela</a></i> do not and instead rely on their built-in vibration sensors.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Spiders of the genus <i>Heptathela</i> have no venom glands although they do have venom gland outlets on the fang tip.<sup id="cite_ref-ForsterPlatnick1984ReviewOfArchaeidsAndRelatives_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForsterPlatnick1984ReviewOfArchaeidsAndRelatives-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The extinct families <a href="/wiki/Arthrolycosidae" title="Arthrolycosidae">Arthrolycosidae</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> rocks, and <a href="/wiki/Arthromygalidae" title="Arthromygalidae">Arthromygalidae</a>, so far found only in <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> rocks, have been classified as members of the Mesothelae.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tarantula_020.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Tarantula_020.jpg/220px-Tarantula_020.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Tarantula_020.jpg/330px-Tarantula_020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Tarantula_020.jpg/440px-Tarantula_020.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1167" data-file-height="918" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tarantula_020.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Mexican red-kneed tarantula <i><a href="/wiki/Brachypelma_smithi" class="mw-redirect" title="Brachypelma smithi">Brachypelma smithi</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mygalomorphae" title="Mygalomorphae">Mygalomorphae</a></div>
<p>The Mygalomorphae, which first appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period,<sup id="cite_ref-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VollrathSelden2007BehaviorInEvolutionOfSpiders-66">[66]</a></sup> are generally heavily built and hairy, with large, robust <a href="/wiki/Chelicera" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelicera">chelicerae</a> and fangs.<sup id="cite_ref-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders-73">[73]</a></sup> Well-known examples include <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantulas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ctenizidae" title="Ctenizidae">ctenizid trapdoor spiders</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Australasian_funnel-web_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Australasian funnel-web spider">Australasian funnel-web spiders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders-8">[8]</a></sup> Most spend the majority of their time in burrows, and some run silk tripwires out from these, but a few build webs to capture prey. However, mygalomorphs cannot produce the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pirifom&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pirifom (page does not exist)">pirifom</a> silk that the Araneomorphae use as instant adhesive to glue silk to surfaces or to other strands of silk, and this makes web construction more difficult for mygalomorphs. Since mygalomorphs rarely "balloon" by using air currents for transport, their populations often form clumps.<sup id="cite_ref-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders-73">[73]</a></sup> In addition to arthropods, mygalomorphs are capable of preying on frogs, small mammals, lizards, and snails.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg/150px-Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg/225px-Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg/300px-Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leucauge_venusta_Kaldari_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Leucauge_venusta" title="Leucauge venusta">Leucauge venusta</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Orb-web_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Orb-web spider">orb-web spider</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Araneomorphae" title="Araneomorphae">Araneomorphae</a></div>
<p>In addition to accounting for over 90% of spider species, the Araneomorphae, also known as the "true spiders", include <a href="/wiki/Orb-web_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Orb-web spider">orb-web spiders</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cursorial" title="Cursorial">cursorial</a> <a href="/wiki/Wolf_spider" title="Wolf spider">wolf spiders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spiders</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders_73-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coddington2005PhylogenyOfSpiders-73">[73]</a></sup> as well as the only known herbivorous spider, <i><a href="/wiki/Bagheera_kiplingi" title="Bagheera kiplingi">Bagheera kiplingi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MeehanOlsonCurry2008VegetarianJumpingSpider-36">[36]</a></sup> They are distinguished by having fangs that oppose each other and cross in a pinching action, in contrast to the Mygalomorphae, which have fangs that are nearly parallel in alignment.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiders_and_people">Spiders and people</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spider_bites">Spider bites</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spider_bite" title="Spider bite">Spider bite</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png/220px-Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png/330px-Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png/440px-Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png 2x" data-file-width="2442" data-file-height="2482" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_Spider_bite.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
All symptoms associated with toxic <a href="/wiki/Spider_bite" title="Spider bite">spider bites</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although spiders are widely feared, only a few species are dangerous to people.<sup id="cite_ref-Vetter_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vetter-82">[82]</a></sup> Spiders will only bite humans in self-defense, and few produce worse effects than a mosquito bite or bee-sting.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Most of those with medically serious bites, such as <a href="/wiki/Recluse_spider" title="Recluse spider">recluse spiders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Widow_spider" class="mw-redirect" title="Widow spider">widow spiders</a>, would rather flee and bite only when trapped, although this can easily arise by accident.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australian_funnel-web_spider" title="Australian funnel-web spider">Funnel web spiders</a>' defensive tactics include fang display and their venom, although they rarely inject much, has resulted in 13 known human deaths over 50 years.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> They have been deemed to be the world's most dangerous spiders on clinical and venom toxicity grounds,<sup id="cite_ref-Vetter_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vetter-82">[82]</a></sup> though this claim has also been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_wandering_spider" title="Brazilian wandering spider">Brazilian wandering spider</a>, due to much more frequent accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were about 100 reliably reported deaths from spider bites in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> compared to about 1,500 from <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> stings.<sup id="cite_ref-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WilliamsonFennerEtAl1996VenomousMarine_Animals-89">[89]</a></sup> Many alleged cases of spider bites may represent incorrect diagnoses,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> which would make it more difficult to check the effectiveness of treatments for genuine bites.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits_to_humans">Benefits to humans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg/220px-Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg/330px-Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg/440px-Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fried_spiders_Skuon_Cambodia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cooked <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantula</a> spiders are considered a delicacy in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spider <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venoms</a> may be a less polluting alternative to conventional <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, as they are deadly to insects but the great majority are harmless to <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. Australian <a href="/wiki/Australian_funnel-web_spider" title="Australian funnel-web spider">funnel web spiders</a> are a promising source, as most of the world's insect pests have had no opportunity to develop any <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> to their venom, and funnel web spiders thrive in captivity and are easy to "milk". It may be possible to target specific pests by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">engineering</a> genes for the production of spider <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> into <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> that infect species such as <a href="/wiki/Cotton_bollworm_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotton bollworm (disambiguation)">cotton bollworms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ch%27ol_people" title="Ch'ol people">Ch'ol Maya</a> use a beverage created from the tarantula species <i><a href="/wiki/Brachypelma_vagans" title="Brachypelma vagans">Brachypelma vagans</a></i> for the treatment of a condition they term 'tarantula wind', the symptoms of which include chest pain, asthma and coughing.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Possible medical uses for spider venoms are being investigated, for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">cardiac arrhythmia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Erectile_dysfunction" title="Erectile dysfunction">erectile dysfunction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The peptide GsMtx-4, found in the venom of <a href="/wiki/Brachypelma_vagans" title="Brachypelma vagans">Brachypelma vagans</a>, is being researched to determine whether or not it could effectively be used for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">cardiac arrhythmia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscular_dystrophy" title="Muscular dystrophy">muscular dystrophy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Glioma" title="Glioma">glioma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Because <a href="/wiki/Spider_silk" title="Spider silk">spider silk</a> is both light and very strong, <a href="/wiki/BioSteel" title="BioSteel">attempts</a> are being made to produce it in <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>' milk and in the leaves of plants, by means of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spiders can also be used as food. Cooked <a href="/wiki/Tarantula" title="Tarantula">tarantula</a> spiders are considered a delicacy in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> and by the <a href="/wiki/Piaroa" class="mw-redirect" title="Piaroa">Piaroa</a> Indians of southern Venezuela&#160;– provided the highly irritant hairs, the spiders' main defense system, are removed first.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arachnophobia">Arachnophobia</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arachnophobia" title="Arachnophobia">Arachnophobia</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arachnophobia" title="Arachnophobia">Arachnophobia</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Specific_phobia" title="Specific phobia">specific phobia</a>—it is the abnormal fear of spiders or anything reminiscent of spiders, such as webs or spider-like shapes. It is one of the most common specific phobias,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and some statistics show that 50% of women and 10% of men show symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> It may be an exaggerated form of an <a href="/wiki/Instinct" title="Instinct">instinctive</a> response that helped early humans to survive,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> or a cultural phenomenon that is most common in predominantly European societies.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiders_in_symbolism_and_culture">Spiders in symbolism and culture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_spiders" title="Cultural depictions of spiders">Cultural depictions of spiders</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ara%C3%B1amuseolarco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ara%C3%B1amuseolarco.jpg/220px-Ara%C3%B1amuseolarco.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Ara%C3%B1amuseolarco.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="188" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ara%C3%B1amuseolarco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This <a href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> ceramic depicts a spider, and dates from around 300 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spiders have been the focus of stories and mythologies of various cultures for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> They have symbolized patience due to their hunting technique of setting webs and waiting for prey, as well as mischief and malice due to their venomous bites.<sup id="cite_ref-Garai73_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garai73-109">[109]</a></sup> The Italian <a href="/wiki/Tarantella" title="Tarantella">tarantella</a> is a dance to rid the young woman of the lustful effects of a spider bite.</p>
<p>Web-spinning also caused the association of the spider with creation myths, as they seem to have the ability to produce their own worlds.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Dreamcatchers are depictions of spiderwebs. The <a href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> people of ancient <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> worshipped nature.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> They placed emphasis on animals and often depicted spiders in their art.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-21"><sup><i><b>v</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-22"><sup><i><b>w</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-23"><sup><i><b>x</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-24"><sup><i><b>y</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-25"><sup><i><b>z</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-26"><sup><i><b>aa</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-27"><sup><i><b>ab</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-28"><sup><i><b>ac</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-29"><sup><i><b>ad</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-30"><sup><i><b>ae</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-31"><sup><i><b>af</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004Spiders_8-32"><sup><i><b>ag</b></i></sup></a> <a
